class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">2041-8205/838/2/L15</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>We report the discovery and spectroscopic confirmation of the quad-like lensed quasar system DES J0408-5354 found in the Dark Energy Survey (DES) Year 1 (Y1) data. This system was discovered during a search for DES Y1 strong lensing systems using a method that identified candidates as red galaxies with multiple blue neighbors. DES J0408-5354 consists of a central red galaxy surrounded by three bright (<IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$i\\lt 20$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/apjlaa624eieqn1.gif\"></IMG>) blue objects and a fourth red object. Subsequent spectroscopic observations using the Gemini South telescope confirmed that the three blue objects are indeed the lensed images of a quasar with redshift <I>z</I>\u00a0=\u00a02.375, and that the central red object is an early-type lensing galaxy with redshift <I>z</I>\u00a0=\u00a00.597. DES J0408-5354 is the first quad lensed quasar system to be found in DES and begins to demonstrate the potential of DES to discover and dramatically increase the sample size of these very rare objects.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/838/2/L15&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aa624e&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/838/2/L15&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aa624e&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...838L..15L\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2017ApJ...838L..15L&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2017ApJ...838L..15L\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/2041-8205/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa624es1\" name=\"apjlaa624es1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong gravitational lensing systems provide valuable tools for studying the properties and evolution of galaxies and quasars, for measuring the distribution of dark matter, and for constraining cosmological parameters. In particular, lensed quasar systems can provide information on, for example, intervening absorption line systems (e.g., Smette et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib32\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib32\">1995</a>), properties of quasar host galaxies (e.g., Peng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib28\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib28\">2006</a>), dark matter substructure in the lens (e.g., Dalal &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib11\">2002</a>), and the stellar content of the lensing galaxy (e.g., Schechter et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib31\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib31\">2014</a>). Moreover, when combined with time delay measurements and careful lens modeling, lensed quasar systems can provide powerful cosmological constraints that are complementary to those of other techniques (e.g., Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib34\">2016</a>; Treu &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib35\">2016</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib7\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib7\">2017</a>).</p><p>Previously, large samples of lensed quasars have been found by surveys such as the Cosmic Lens All-Sky Survey (Browne et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib9\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib9\">2003</a>; Myers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib24\">2003</a>) in the radio and the Sloan Digital Sky Survey Quasar Lens Search (SQLS; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib26\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib26\">2006</a>; Inada et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib18\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib18\">2012</a>) and the SDSS-III BOSS quasar lens survey (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib22\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib22\">2016</a>) in the optical. The Dark Energy Survey (DES; Dark Energy Survey Collaboration <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib12\">2005</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib13\">2016</a>) is an ongoing imaging survey covering 5000\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> of the Southern Galactic Cap in the <em>grizY</em> filters using the Dark Energy Camera (Flaugher et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib17\">2015</a>), and it holds the promise of significantly increasing the numbers of lensed quasars. In particular, based on the forecasts of Oguri &amp; Marshall (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib25\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib25\">2010</a>), we expect to find in DES about 120 lensed quasar systems brighter than <em>i</em>\u00a0=\u00a021 (magnitude limit applies to the fainter image for pairs and third brightest image for quadruple systems, or quads; see Figure 1 of Ostrovski et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib27\">2017</a>). DES, specifically with the external collaboration project STRIDES<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjlaa624efn1\">55</a></sup>\n (PI: T. Treu), aims to use the resulting large lensed quasar sample for the primary science goal of improving constraints on cosmological parameters. This lensed quasar sample is predicted to include about 20 of the very rare quad systems that will provide additional valuable information compared to pair systems for constraining lens models (specifically extra constraints from two more positions and two more time delays), in particular for cosmology purposes (e.g., Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib33\">2013</a>) and substructure studies (e.g., Dalal &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib11\">2002</a>).</p><p>To date, we have discovered and spectroscopically confirmed three lensed quasar pair systems (Agnello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib3\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib3\">2015b</a>; Ostrovski et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib27\">2017</a>) in DES. Here in this Letter we report the discovery and confirmation of the first lensed quasar quad (or quad-like) system, DES J0408-5354, in the DES. We first describe our lensed quasar search procedure, system discovery, and photometric data in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa624es2\">2</a>. We then describe our spectroscopic observations and present our data in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa624es3\">3</a>. We summarize and conclude in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa624es4\">4</a>. Detailed photometry analysis and lens modeling for this system are presented in a modeling paper, Agnello et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>), to which we will refer the reader where relevant below.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa624es2\" name=\"apjlaa624es2\">2.\u00a0Search and Discovery</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The system DES J0408-5354 was discovered during a systematic visual search for candidate strong lensing systems in the DES Year 1 (Y1) data (Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib16\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib16\">2014</a>). A number of different search methods have been applied to the DES Y1 data, but the specific method involved in this case was a \"blue-near-red\" technique, where we first automatically identified candidate systems of red galaxies with multiple neighboring blue objects within some radius, and then used visual inspections of these systems to rate them and to select the best candidates for subsequent spectroscopic follow-up. This method has been used previously in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) data to search for bright strong lensing systems and then to confirm them spectroscopically with good success (e.g., Belokurov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib5\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib5\">2009</a>; Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib15\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib15\">2009</a>).</p><p>Here, we started with a DES Y1 red galaxy sample selected using the redMaGiC technique (Rozo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib30\">2016</a>). To minimize stellar contamination of this sample, we rejected objects with <tt>SExtractor</tt> (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib6\">1996</a>) <tt>spread</tt>_<tt>model</tt>(Desai et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib14\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib14\">2012</a>; Bouy et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib8\">2013</a>) values\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn2.gif\" alt=\"$\\leqslant 0.01$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\leqslant 0.01</script></span></span>. We then identified our initial set of candidates as those redMaGiC galaxies with three or more blue objects within a radius <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn3.gif\" alt=\"$\\lt 10^{\\prime\\prime} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\lt 10^{\\prime\\prime}</script></span></span>, where we defined a blue object as one with colors <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$-1\\lt =\\,g-r\\lt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">-1\\lt =\\,g-r\\lt 1</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$-1\\lt =\\,r-i\\lt 1$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">-1\\lt =\\,r-i\\lt 1</script></span></span>. We did not apply any star/galaxy separation cut to the blue objects, but did reject objects that are saturated in any of the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn6.gif\" alt=\"$g,r,i$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g,r,i</script></span></span> filters using a <tt>SExtractor flags</tt>\u00a0<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn7.gif\" alt=\"$\\lt \\,=\\,3$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\lt \\,=\\,3</script></span></span> cut. We also applied a magnitude cut <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn8.gif\" alt=\"$r\\lt 22$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">r\\lt 22</script></span></span> on the blue objects to keep the number of candidates manageable for the visual inspection step and to have relatively brighter candidates to ease follow-up spectroscopic redshift measurements.</p><p>Applying the above criteria resulted in a list of 6526 systems that one of us (HL) then inspected visually by examining their DES <em>gri</em> color composite images. The discovery image of DES J0408-5354 is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a>, where we see a central red galaxy (G1) surrounded by three blue objects (A, B, and D) and a fourth red object (G2) (objects labeled as in Agnello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>). This system stood out as a potential quadruply lensed quasar system, except that the fourth putative lensed quasar image has a conspicuously redder color than those of the other three images. Regardless, the quad-like configuration of DES J0408-5354 made it a very good candidate for subsequent spectroscopy, described in the next section. We present a detailed lensing model for this system in Agnello et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>), which concludes that the system is indeed a quad where the reddened image is a blend of the fourth lensed quasar image with a perturbing galaxy.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa624ef1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa624ef1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0DES <em>gri</em> color composite discovery image (top left), <tt>SExtractor</tt> segmentation map plotted over the DES <em>i</em>-band coadded image (bottom left), Gemini <em>i</em>-band acquisition image (top right), and the spectroscopic slit layout (bottom right). The central red lensing galaxy is G1; the three blue lensed quasar images are A, B, and D; a fourth red image is G2; and the redMaGiC galaxy is G0. The 5 components (A, B, D, G1, G2) of the system are not fully separated in the DES catalog, with D+G1 and B+G2 remaining blended.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa624ef1\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa624ef1\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>In the DES Y1 catalog, DES J0408-5354 is composed of three objects due to the fact that components D and G1 are blended and that B and G2 are also blended. As such, the catalog contains entries from A, D+G1, and B+G2. The <tt>SExtractor</tt> segmentation map for the system is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a>, overplotted on the DES <em>i</em>-band image of the system. We need to point out here that all three catalog objects actually meet our blue object color criteria given before (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef2\">2</a>, leftmost panel), so that the redMaGiC galaxy for this system is not G1 but rather the red galaxy G0 marked in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a>. G0 is about <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn9.gif\" alt=\"$6^{\\prime\\prime} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">6^{\\prime\\prime}</script></span></span> away from G1 and has a redMaGiC photometric redshift of 0.66\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03, close to G1's spectroscopic redshift of 0.597 (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaa624es3\">3</a>). Therefore, in this case, our blue-near-red method found the system via another red galaxy likely associated with the lensing galaxy, rather than directly via the red lensing galaxy itself. The discovery was thus likely not coincidental, but was also not by the direct route as intended by the method. In similar DES Y1 blue-near-red and related searches, we also see other cases in galaxy group and cluster environments where the lensing system is being indirectly found this way (H. T. Diehl et al. 2017, in preparation).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa624ef2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa624ef2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Color\u2013color plots showing components A (pink stars), D+G1 (gray stars), and B+G2 (yellow stars) in different color spaces. We did not assign <em>W</em>1 or <em>W</em>2 fluxes to each individual component, so values show the sum of all flux in those bands. For comparison, the color loci of quasars (green), point sources (blue), and extended sources (orange) are populated by the objects present in the GMM training set.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa624ef2\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa624ef2\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The coordinates and photometry of the three DES catalog objects are given in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa624et1\">1</a>. The DES <tt>SExtractor auto</tt> magnitudes of the three catalog objects are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa624et1\">1</a>, as are the near-infrared Kron magnitudes (Kron <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib19\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib19\">1980</a>) from the VISTA Hemisphere Survey (VHS; McMahon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib21\">2013</a>) and the mid-infrared magnitudes from the <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> (<em>WISE</em>; Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib36\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib36\">2010</a>). For the near-infrared catalog, magnitudes were obtained by cross-matching the DES and VHS catalogs using a 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 search radius. Component D+G1 does not have a <em>J</em>-band magnitude value due to blending with B+G2 in this band. For the mid-infrared magnitudes, the DES and <em>WISE</em> catalogs were cross-matched using a 4<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />0 search radius. All components of the system are blended into a single <em>WISE</em> source, so that the <em>W</em>1 and <em>W</em>2 magnitudes displayed in the table apply to A+D+G1+B+G2 as a whole.</p><div rowspan=\"1\">\n\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\"><em>W</em>1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">16.78\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\"><em>W</em>2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">16.51\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\"\u00b1\">\u00a0</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/suppdata/apjlaa624et1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aa624e\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/suppdata/apjlaa624et1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We present in Agnello et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>) a detailed analysis that provides the deblended positions and <em>grizY</em> magnitudes of all five components A, B, D, G1, and G2 in this system. For completeness we also provide this information in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaa624et1\">1</a>, as well as include the DES catalog position and photometry for galaxy G0.</p><p>After DES J0408-5354 was first announced to the STRIDES Collaboration as a lensed quasar candidate from the blue-near-red search, a number of other search methods within STRIDES were examined and also seen to identify the system as a candidate. These other search methods are described in more detail in Agnello et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>). We provide here a brief summary: (1) the Gaussian Mixture Model (GMM) method of Ostrovski et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib27\">2017</a>), which uses supervised machine learning in a five-dimensional optical plus infrared color space and identified DES J0408-5354 as a candidate pair (D+G1 not found separately in <em>J</em> band due to blending noted above); (2) CHITAH (Chan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib10\">2015</a>), which uses pixel-based automatic recognition on <em>grizY</em> cutout images and identified the system as a candidate pair (not flagged as a quad because the fourth image G2 is too red); and (3) the Artificial Neural Network method of Agnello et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib2\">2015a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib3\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib3\">2015b</a>), which uses <em>griz</em> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn10.gif\" alt=\"$W1W2$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">W1W2</script></span></span> magnitudes and identified DES J0408-5354 as a candidate extended quasar. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef2\">2</a> shows how the system was flagged by the GMM method as a candidate, due to the quasar-like colors of its components. We note that none of the search methods mentioned in this Letter selected DES J0408-5354 as a <em>quad</em> candidate, due to blending issues and/or reddended colors. The unusual color and configuration of this system and potential others like it argue for incorporating more flexibility into our search techniques (Agnello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa624es3\" name=\"apjlaa624es3\">3.\u00a0Spectroscopic Observations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Spectroscopic observations of DES J0408-5354 were carried out using the Gemini Multi-Object Spectrograph (GMOS-S) on the Gemini South telescope, as part of a larger Gemini Large and Long Program (LLP; PI: E. Buckley-Geer; program IDs GS-2015B-LP-5, GS-2016A-LP-5) of spectroscopic follow-up for DES strong lensing systems and for DES photometric redshift (photo-<em>z</em>) calibrations. GMOS-S was used in multi-object spectroscopy (MOS) mode on 2015 December 9 UT to take spectra of the three blue objects A, B, and D. The <em>i</em>-band GMOS-S acquisition image is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a> (top right). A single slit mask was used, with one slit for A and a second slit for B and D together; see blue slits in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a> (bottom right). (Another 38 slits were assigned to unrelated DES photo-<em>z</em> calibration targets.) Two sets of spectra were taken: blue data using the B600 grating (dispersion <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn11.gif\" alt=\"$\\approx 1.0$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\approx 1.0</script></span></span> \u00c5\u00a0pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>) and red data using the R400 grating (dispersion <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn12.gif\" alt=\"$\\approx 1.5$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\approx 1.5</script></span></span> \u00c5\u00a0pixel<sup>\u22121</sup>). We used 4\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0900 s exposures for cosmic ray rejection and processed the data using the IRAF Gemini reduction package. The seeing was 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />8, measured from spectra of mask-alignment stars in the science data.</p><p>From the extracted (but not flux calibrated) 1D spectra shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef3\">3</a> (top panels), we clearly see that all three blue images A, B, and D show strong quasar emission lines at the same redshift, specifically Ly<em>\u03b1</em> 1216 \u00c5 and C <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> 1549 \u00c5 in the blue spectra, and C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] 1909 \u00c5 and Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> 2800 \u00c5 in the red spectra. From the C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] line, which shows the cleanest, most symmetric line profile among these four broad emission features, we use the <tt>emsao</tt> task in the IRAF external package <tt>rvsao</tt> (Kurtz &amp; Mink <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib20\">1998</a>) to report a redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.375 for the quasar.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa624ef3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa624ef3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0(Top panels) Blue (top left) and red (top right) Gemini GMOS-S spectra for the three blue images A (red), B (black), and D (blue). (Bottom panels) Fractional differences between the blue (bottom left) and red (bottom right) spectra of A (red) or D (blue) relative to B. The median counts in the spectra of A and D have been rescaled to match those of B, over the respective blue and red wavelength ranges shown.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa624ef3\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa624ef3\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>To compare the spectra of the three blue images in more detail, we also show in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef3\">3</a> (bottom panels) the fractional differences between the blue and red spectra of images A and D relative to those of image B, which has the spectra with the most counts. We first rescale the spectra of A and D so that they each have the same median counts as those of B over the respective blue and red wavelength ranges plotted. The results in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef3\">3</a> show generally good agreement among the spectra, especially for the red spectra. For the blue spectra, we do see an overall slope for the spectrum of A relative to that of B; we attribute this slope to extinction differences between the lines of sight to these two images, and/or to differences in atmospheric differential refraction between the two differently oriented slits (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a>) used to observe images A and B. In addition, we also see conspicuous differences between the spectra of D and B at the locations of the strong Ly<em>\u03b1</em> and C <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> emission lines, and to a lesser extent at the C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] line. We attribute these differences to the effects of microlensing by stars in the lensing galaxy, manifested as flux ratio differences, in pairs of lensed quasar images, for the continuum versus the emission line regions in the spectra (e.g., Motta et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib23\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib23\">2012</a>). Microlensing may also cause the spectral slope differences mentioned above.</p><p>Subsequent to these observations that confirmed the three blue images as having the same quasar redshift, we designed a second MOS slit mask targeting the central lensing galaxy G1 and the fourth image G2, each allocated to a single slit. The slits for G1 and G2 are shown in red in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a> (bottom right). Red R400 observations were obtained in the Gemini South semester 2016A observing queue, on 2016 April 9, under 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 seeing conditions and using the same observing setup as above. Blue B600 observations were also put into the queue, but no data were obtained before the system set in 2016A. We show the R400 spectra in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef4\">4</a>, where we see that G2 shows broad C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] and Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> emission at the same redshift as found for the three blue images, while G1 shows Ca H+K absorption lines, indicating that G1 is an early-type lensing galaxy with redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.597.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaa624ef4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaa624ef4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624ef4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Red Gemini GMOS-S spectra of the fourth image G2 (top panel, red) and of the central lensing galaxy G1 (bottom panel, red). In both panels, the black spectrum is that of image B.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaa624ef4\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaa624ef4\" href=\"/2041-8205/838/2/L15/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaa624ef4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>However, we also see that the G1 spectrum unexpectedly shows the same C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission line as in the lensed quasar spectra, leading us to suspect there is contaminating light from the neighboring lensed quasar image D, which is only about 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3 away (cf. the 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 seeing). Likewise, object B, the brightest quasar image, is only about 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 away from the substantially fainter object G2, suggesting that the C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] line seen in the G2 spectrum is also contamination from object B. Using the image modeling code <tt>GALFIT</tt> (Peng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib29\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib29\">2010</a>), we simulate image B as a Moffat profile with an FWHM of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 and find that about 12% of its light falls within the 1''-wide slit and 2''-long spectral extraction aperture used for G2. Though seemingly small, this 12% fraction actually amounts to about 80% of the <em>r</em>-band light from G2 itself, given that <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn13.gif\" alt=\"$r({\\rm{B}})=19.95$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">r({\\rm{B}})=19.95</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/838/2/L15/Full/apjlaa624eieqn14.gif\" alt=\"$r({\\rm{G}}2)=21.98$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">r({\\rm{G}}2)=21.98</script></span></span> from Table 1 (Agnello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>). (We use <em>r</em> band as the C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] line falls within that filter.) This estimated contamination is thus substantial and unfortunately we are unable to correct for it as image B lies off the slit for G2 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaa624ef1\">1</a>, bottom right) and we do not have a concurrent spectrum of B. We thus cannot rule out contamination from B as the source of the C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] line seen in the G2 spectrum (likewise for D and G1) and will need future observations to verify whether G2 indeed shows quasar emission features or not.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaa624es4\" name=\"apjlaa624es4\">4.\u00a0Summary and Conclusions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>DES J0408-5354 was identified as a candidate lensed quasar quad system from the DES first-year data. Subsequent follow-up spectroscopy confirmed the three bright blue objects in this system to be the images of a quasar at redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.375, lensed by an early-type red galaxy with redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.597. Another reddened object in the system, possibly a blend of a perturbing galaxy and the fourth lensed quasar image, was also observed spectroscopically, though without conclusive results.</p><p>Our modeling paper, Agnello et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib4\">2017</a>), presents a detailed model of the system, from which we expect the longest time delay in the system to be about 80 days, making DES J0408-5354 well suited (Treu &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib35\">2016</a>) for time delay measurements, which we are undertaking via a monitoring campaign within the STRIDES Collaboration. The lensing model also constrains the mass of the possible perturbing galaxy and thus provides information about substructure in the lensing mass distribution. Further spectroscopic follow-up and high-resolution imaging data should provide more needed details about the main lensing galaxy and its environment. We thus expect this quad system to be particularly useful for the application of time delay cosmography (e.g., Treu &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib35\">2016</a>) and substructure studies (e.g., Dalal &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaa624ebib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaa624ebib11\">2002</a>). DES J0408-5354 heralds a much larger sample of some 20 of these very rare and valuable quad lensed quasar systems anticipated to be discovered by the DES.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Funding for the DES Projects has been provided by the U.S. Department of Energy, the U.S. National Science Foundation, the Ministry of Science and Education of Spain, the Science and Technology Facilities Council of the United Kingdom, the Higher Education Funding Council for England, the National Center for Supercomputing Applications at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Kavli Institute of Cosmological Physics at the University of Chicago, the Center for Cosmology and Astro-Particle Physics at the Ohio State University, the Mitchell Institute for Fundamental Physics and Astronomy at Texas A&amp;M University, Financiadora de Estudos e Projetos, Funda\u00e7\u00e3o Carlos Chagas Filho de Amparo \u00e0 Pesquisa do Estado do Rio de Janeiro, Conselho Nacional de Desenvolvimento Cient\u00edfico e Tecnol\u00f3gico and the Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia, Tecnologia e Inova\u00e7\u00e3o, the Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft and the Collaborating Institutions in the Dark Energy Survey.</p><p>The Collaborating Institutions are Argonne National Laboratory, the University of California at Santa Cruz, the University of Cambridge, Centro de Investigaciones Energ\u00e9ticas, Medioambientales y Tecnol\u00f3gicas-Madrid, the University of Chicago, University College London, the DES-Brazil Consortium, the University of Edinburgh, the Eidgen\u00f6ssische Technische Hochschule (ETH) Z\u00fcrich, Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory, the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Institut de Ci\u00e8ncies de l'Espai (IEEC/CSIC), the Institut de F\u00edsica d'Altes Energies, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, the Ludwig-Maximilians Universit\u00e4t M\u00fcnchen and the associated Excellence Cluster Universe, the University of Michigan, the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, the University of Nottingham, The Ohio State University, the University of Pennsylvania, the University of Portsmouth, SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory, Stanford University, the University of Sussex, Texas A&amp;M University, and the OzDES Membership Consortium.</p><p>The DES data management system is supported by the National Science Foundation under grant number AST-1138766. The DES participants from Spanish institutions are partially supported by MINECO under grants AYA2012-39559, ESP2013-48274, FPA2013-47986, and Centro de Excelencia Severo Ochoa SEV-2012-0234. Research leading to these results has received funding from the European Research Council under the European Union's Seventh Framework Programme FP7/2007-2013) including ERC grant agreements 240672, 291329, and 306478.</p><p>Based on observations obtained at the Gemini Observatory (acquired through the Gemini Observatory Archive and processed using the Gemini IRAF package), which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., under a cooperative agreement with the NSF on behalf of the Gemini partnership: the National Science Foundation (United States), the National Research Council (Canada), CONICYT (Chile), Ministerio de Ciencia, Tecnolog\u00eda e Innovaci\u00f3n Productiva (Argentina), and Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia, Tecnologia e Inova\u00e7\u00e3o (Brazil).</p><p>The analysis presented here is based on observations obtained as part of the VISTA Hemisphere Survey, ESO Programme, 179.A-2010 (PI: McMahon).</p></div></div><!-- This system was discovered during a search for DES Y1 strong lensing systems using a method that identified candidates as red galaxies with multiple blue neighbors. DES J0408-5354 consists of a central red galaxy surrounded by three bright (I&lt; 20) blue objects and a fourth red object. Subsequent spectroscopic observations using the Gemini South telescope confirmed that the three blue objects are indeed the lensed images of a quasar with redshift z = 2.375, and that the central red object is an early-type lensing galaxy with redshift z = 0.597. DES J0408-5354 is the first quad lensed quasar system to be found in DES and begins to demonstrate the potential of DES to discover and dramatically increase the sample size of these very rare objects.", "doi": "10.3847/2041-8213/aa624e", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1702.00072", "issn": "0004-637X", "number": "2"}, "Inspection status": "Complete"}
